<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Blue</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A primary colour between purple and green</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the colour. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Blue_(disambiguation)" title="Blue (disambiguation)">Blue (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

(byte)<br/></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Blue</b> is one of the three <a href="/wiki/Primary_color" title="Primary color">primary colours</a> of pigments in painting and traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_theory" title="Colour theory">colour theory</a>, as well as in the <a href="/wiki/RGB_color_model" title="RGB color model">RGB colour model</a>. It lies between <a href="/wiki/Violet_(color)" title="Violet (color)">violet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green" title="Green">green</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_spectrum" title="Optical spectrum">spectrum</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>. The eye perceives blue when observing light with a <a href="/wiki/Dominant_wavelength" title="Dominant wavelength">dominant wavelength</a> between approximately 450 and 495 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nanometres</a>.  Most blues contain a slight mixture of other colors; <a href="/wiki/Azure_(color)" title="Azure (color)">azure</a> contains some green, while <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a> contains some violet. The clear daytime sky and the deep sea appear blue because of an optical effect known as <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering#Cause_of_the_blue_color_of_the_sky" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a>.  An optical effect called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyndall_scattering" title="Tyndall scattering">Tyndall scattering</a> explains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>. Distant objects appear more blue because of another optical effect called <a href="/wiki/Aerial_perspective" title="Aerial perspective">aerial perspective</a>.
</p><p>Blue has been an important colour in art and decoration since ancient times. The semi-precious stone <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> was used in ancient Egypt for jewellery and ornament and later, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Renaissance" title="The Renaissance">the Renaissance</a>, to make the pigment <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, the most expensive of all pigments. In the eighth century Chinese artists used <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a> to colour fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white porcelain</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, European artists used it in the windows of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedrals" title="Cathedrals">Cathedrals</a>.  Europeans wore clothing coloured with the vegetable dye <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a> until it was replaced by the finer <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a> from America.  In the 19th century, synthetic blue dyes and pigments gradually replaced mineral pigments and synthetic dyes.  Dark blue became a common colour for military uniforms and later, in the late 20th century, for business suits.  Because blue has commonly been associated with harmony, it was chosen as the colour of the flags of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Surveys in the US and Europe show that blue is the colour most commonly associated with harmony, faithfulness, confidence, distance, infinity, the imagination, cold, and sometimes with sadness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeller200924_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeller200924-3">[3]</a></sup> In US and European public opinion polls it is the most popular colour, chosen by almost half of both men and women as their favourite colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeller200922_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeller200922-4">[4]</a></sup> The same surveys also showed that blue was the colour most associated with the masculine, just ahead of black, and was also the colour most associated with intelligence, knowledge, calm and concentration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeller200924_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeller200924-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shades_and_variations">Shades and variations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shades_of_blue" title="Shades of blue">Shades of blue</a></div>
<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linear_visible_spectrum.svg" title="Blue is between violet and green in the spectrum of visible light"><img alt="Blue is between violet and green in the spectrum of visible light" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="605" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/400px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/600px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/800px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div></div>
<p>Blue is the colour of light between violet and green on the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. Hues of blue include indigo and <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, closer to violet; pure blue, without any mixture of other colours; <a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">Cyan</a>, which is midway in the spectrum between blue and green, and the other blue-greens <a href="/wiki/Turquoise_(color)" title="Turquoise (color)">turquoise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teal" title="Teal">teal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aquamarine_(color)" title="Aquamarine (color)">aquamarine</a>.
</p><p>Blue also varies in shade or tint; darker shades of blue contain black or grey, while lighter tints contain white.  Darker shades of blue include ultramarine, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Navy_blue" title="Navy blue">navy blue</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a>; while lighter tints include <a href="/wiki/Sky_blue" title="Sky blue">sky blue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azure_(color)" title="Azure (color)">azure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_blue" title="Egyptian blue">Egyptian blue</a>. (For a more complete list see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_colors" title="List of colors">List of colours</a>).
</p><p>Blue pigments were originally made from minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>, and blue dyes were made from plants; usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a> in Europe, and <i><a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">Indigofera tinctoria</a></i>, or true indigo, in Asia and Africa. Today most blue pigments and dyes are made by a chemical process.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Pure blue, also known as high blue, is not mixed with any other colours
</p>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_linguistic_differences">Etymology and linguistic differences</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">modern English</a> word <i>blue</i> comes from <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>bleu</i> or <i>blewe</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>bleu</i>, a word of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_language" title="Germanic language">Germanic</a> origin, related to the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> word <i>blao</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_1970_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster_1970-5">[5]</a></sup> In heraldry, the word <a href="/wiki/Azure_(heraldry)" title="Azure (heraldry)">azure</a> is used for blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Russian and some other languages, there is no single word for blue, but rather different words for light blue (голубой, goluboy) and dark blue (синий, siniy). See <a href="/wiki/Color_term" title="Color term">Colour term</a>.
</p><p>Several languages, including <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Language" title="Korean Language">Korean</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lakota_Sioux" title="Lakota Sioux">Lakota Sioux</a>, use the same word to describe blue and green. For example, in Vietnamese the colour of both tree leaves and the sky is <i>xanh</i>. In Japanese, the word for blue (青 ao) is often used for colours that English speakers would refer to as green, such as the colour of a traffic signal meaning "go". (For more on this subject, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distinguishing_blue_from_green_in_language" title="Distinguishing blue from green in language">Distinguishing blue from green in language</a>)
</p><p>Linguistic research indicates that languages do not begin by having a word for the colour blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup>  Colour names often developed individually in natural languages, typically beginning with <a href="/wiki/Black" title="Black">black</a> and <a href="/wiki/White" title="White">white</a> (or dark and light), and then adding <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a>, and only much later – usually as the last main category of colour accepted in a language – adding the colour blue, probably when blue pigments could be manufactured reliably in the culture using that language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_nature">Science and nature</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; width:400px; text-align:center; margin:0.5em auto; width:auto; margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="3" style="background:#FFF;"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linear_visible_spectrum.svg" title="sRGB rendering of the spectrum of visible light"><img alt="sRGB rendering of the spectrum of visible light" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="605" decoding="async" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/200px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/300px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Linear_visible_spectrum.svg/400px-Linear_visible_spectrum.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Color" title="Color">Colour</a>

</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td style="background:#f4b0b0;"><b><a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a></b>

</td>
<td>620–750 nm
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Human eyes perceive blue when observing light which has a <a href="/wiki/Dominant_wavelength" title="Dominant wavelength">dominant wavelength</a> of roughly 450–495 nanometres. Blues with a higher frequency and thus a shorter wavelength gradually look more violet, while those with a lower frequency and a longer wavelength gradually appear more green. Pure blue, in the middle, has a wavelength of 470 nanometres.
</p><p>Isaac Newton included blue as one of the seven colours in his first description the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>,  He chose seven colours because that was the number of notes in the musical scale, which he believed was related to the optical spectrum.  He included <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a>, the hue between blue and violet, as one of the separate colours, though today it is usually considered a hue of blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In painting and traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_theory" title="Colour theory">colour theory</a>, blue is one of the three <a href="/wiki/Primary_color" title="Primary color">primary colours</a> of pigments (red, yellow, blue), which can be mixed to form a wide <a href="/wiki/Gamut" title="Gamut">gamut</a> of colours.  Red and blue mixed together form violet, blue and yellow together form green.  Mixing all three primary colours together produces a dark grey. From the Renaissance onwards, painters used this system to create their colours. (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RYB" title="RYB">RYB</a> colour system.)
</p><p>The RYB model was used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_printing" title="Colour printing">colour printing</a> by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Christoph_Le_Blon" title="Jacob Christoph Le Blon">Jacob Christoph Le Blon</a> as early as 1725. Later, printers discovered that more accurate colours could be created by using combinations of magenta, cyan, yellow and black ink, put onto separate inked plates and then overlaid one at a time onto paper. This method could produce almost all the colours in the spectrum with reasonable accuracy.
</p><p>In the 19th century the Scottish physicist <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> found a new way of explaining colours, by the wavelength of their light. He showed that white light could be created by combining red, blue and green light, and that virtually all colours could be made by different combinations of these three colours. His idea, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Additive_colour" title="Additive colour">additive colour</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RGB_colour_model" title="RGB colour model">RGB colour model</a>, is used today to create colours on televisions and computer screens. The screen is covered by tiny pixels, each with three fluorescent elements for creating red, green and blue light. If the red, blue and green elements all glow at once, the pixel looks white. As the screen is scanned from behind with electrons, each pixel creates its own designated colour, composing a complete picture on the screen.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RGB_illumination.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/RGB_illumination.jpg" width="267"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Additive colour mixing. The projection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_colour" title="Primary colour">primary colour</a> lights on a screen shows secondary colours where two overlap; the combination red, green, and blue each in full intensity makes white.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 251.33333333333px"><div style="width: 251.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 249.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RGB_pixels.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/RGB_pixels.jpg/374px-RGB_pixels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/RGB_pixels.jpg/560px-RGB_pixels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/RGB_pixels.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Blue and orange pixels on an LCD television screen. Closeup of the red, green and blue sub-pixels on left.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>On the <a href="/wiki/HSL_and_HSV" title="HSL and HSV">HSV colour wheel</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_color" title="Complementary color">complement</a> of blue is <a href="/wiki/Yellow" title="Yellow">yellow</a>; that is, a colour corresponding to an equal mixture of <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green" title="Green">green</a> light. On a colour wheel based on traditional colour theory (<a href="/wiki/RYB_color_model" title="RYB color model">RYB</a>) where blue was considered a primary colour, its complementary colour is considered to be <a href="/wiki/Orange_(colour)" title="Orange (colour)">orange</a> (based on the <a href="/wiki/Munsell_color_system" title="Munsell color system">Munsell colour wheel</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigments_and_dyes">Pigments and dyes</span></h3>
<p>Blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigments" title="Pigments">pigments</a> were made from minerals, especially <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Cu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>(CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>)</span>.  These minerals were crushed, ground into powder, and then mixed with a quick-drying binding agent, such as egg yolk (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tempera_painting" title="Tempera painting">tempera painting</a>); or with a slow-drying oil, such as <a href="/wiki/Linseed_oil" title="Linseed oil">linseed oil</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Oil_painting" title="Oil painting">oil painting</a>. To make blue <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a> (cobalt(II) aluminate: <span class="chemf nowrap">CoAl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>)pigment was mixed with the glass. Other common blue pigments made from minerals are <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<sub>8-10</sub>Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sub></span>S<sub>2-4</sub></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerulean_blue" title="Cerulean blue">cerulean blue</a> (primarily cobalt (II) stanate: <span class="chemf nowrap">Co<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SnO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a> (milori blue: primarily <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span>(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sub></span></span>).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_dyes" title="Natural dyes">Natural dyes</a> to colour cloth and tapestries were made from plants. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">Woad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">true indigo</a> were used to produce <a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">indigo dye</a> used to colour fabrics blue or <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a>. Since the 18th century, natural blue dyes have largely been replaced by synthetic dyes.
</p><p><span id="Reflex_Blue"></span>	
"Reflex blue" used to be the name of a common blue pigment in ink manufacturing. 	
In the 1960s, the name was adopted into the proprietary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantone_Matching_System" title="Pantone Matching System">Pantone Matching System</a> (PMS) to refer to this specific pigment. Pantone "Reflex Blue" has the particularity of being identified only by this name, and not by a number code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 184px"><div style="width: 184px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 182px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1826" data-file-width="2214" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg/273px-Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg/410px-Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg/546px-Lapis-lazuli_hg.jpg 2x" width="182"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">Lapis lazuli</a>, mined in Afghanistan for more than three thousand years, was used for jewellery and ornaments, and later was crushed and powdered and used as a pigment. The more it was ground, the lighter the blue colour became.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 194.66666666667px"><div style="width: 194.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 192.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azurite_from_China.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1672" data-file-width="2143" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Azurite_from_China.jpg/289px-Azurite_from_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Azurite_from_China.jpg/433px-Azurite_from_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Azurite_from_China.jpg/577px-Azurite_from_China.jpg 2x" width="193"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">Azurite</a>, common in Europe and Asia, is produced by the weathering of copper ore deposits.  It was crushed and powdered and used as a pigment from ancient times,
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg/270px-Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg/406px-Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Natural_ultramarine_pigment.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Natural <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, made by grinding and purifying lapis lazuli, was the finest available blue pigment in the Middle Ages and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. It was extremely expensive, and in <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> art, it was often reserved for the robes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_blue.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="456" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Egyptian_blue.jpg/270px-Egyptian_blue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Egyptian_blue.jpg/406px-Egyptian_blue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Egyptian_blue.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_blue" title="Egyptian blue">Egyptian blue</a>, the first artificial pigment, created in the third millennium BC in Ancient Egypt by grinding sand, copper and <a href="/wiki/Natron" title="Natron">natron</a>, and then heating them. It was often used in tomb paintings and funereal objects to protect the dead in their afterlife.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Azuritepigment.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azuritepigment.jpg/300px-Azuritepigment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azuritepigment.jpg/451px-Azuritepigment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Azuritepigment.jpg/600px-Azuritepigment.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Ground azurite was often in Renaissance used as a substitute for the much more expensive lapis lazuli. It made a rich blue, but was unstable and could turn dark green over time.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1532" data-file-width="1532" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG/225px-PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG/338px-PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG/450px-PB35_Bleu_C%C3%A9rul%C3%A9um.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Cerulean" title="Cerulean">Cerulean</a> was created with copper and <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_oxide" title="Cobalt oxide">cobalt oxide</a>, and used to make a sky blue colour.  Like azurite, it could fade or turn green.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 178px"><div style="width: 178px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 176px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cobalt_Blue.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="685" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cobalt_Blue.JPG/264px-Cobalt_Blue.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cobalt_Blue.JPG/396px-Cobalt_Blue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cobalt_Blue.JPG/527px-Cobalt_Blue.JPG 2x" width="176"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">Cobalt blue</a>. Cobalt has been used for centuries to colour glass and ceramics; it was used to make the deep blue <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> windows of Gothic cathedrals and Chinese <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> beginning in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%27ang_Dynasty" title="T'ang Dynasty">T'ang Dynasty</a>. In 1799 a French chemist, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Louis Jacques Thénard</a>, made a synthetic cobalt blue pigment which became immensely popular with painters.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 153.33333333333px"><div style="width: 153.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 151.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigo_plant_extract_sample.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="228" data-file-width="230" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Indigo_plant_extract_sample.jpg/227px-Indigo_plant_extract_sample.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Indigo_plant_extract_sample.jpg 1.5x" width="152"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">Indigo dye</a> is made from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">Indigofera tinctoria</a></i>, a plant common in Asia and Africa but little known in Europe until the 15th century. Its importation into Europe revolutionised the colour of clothing. It also became the colour used in blue <a href="/wiki/Denim" title="Denim">denim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeans" title="Jeans">jeans</a>.  Nearly all indigo dye produced today is synthetic.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 271.33333333333px"><div style="width: 271.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 269.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigo_skeletal.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="287" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Indigo_skeletal.svg/404px-Indigo_skeletal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Indigo_skeletal.svg/607px-Indigo_skeletal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Indigo_skeletal.svg/808px-Indigo_skeletal.svg.png 2x" width="270"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Chemical structure of <a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">indigo dye</a>, a widely produced blue dye. <a href="/wiki/Jeans" title="Jeans">Blue jeans</a> consist of 1–3% by weight of this <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compound</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ultramarinepigment.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ultramarinepigment.jpg/300px-Ultramarinepigment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ultramarinepigment.jpg/451px-Ultramarinepigment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ultramarinepigment.jpg/600px-Ultramarinepigment.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Synthetic <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a> pigment, invented in 1826, has the same chemical composition as natural ultramarine. It is more vivid than natural ultramarine because the particles are smaller and more uniform in size, and thus distribute the light more evenly.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bleu_phtalo.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1218" data-file-width="1218" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bleu_phtalo.jpg/225px-Bleu_phtalo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bleu_phtalo.jpg/338px-Bleu_phtalo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Bleu_phtalo.jpg/450px-Bleu_phtalo.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A new synthetic blue created in the 1930s is <a href="/wiki/Phthalocyanine" title="Phthalocyanine">phthalocyanine</a>, an intense colour widely used for making blue ink, <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dye</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 181.33333333333px"><div style="width: 181.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 179.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1149" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg/269px-YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg/405px-YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg/539px-YInMn_Blue_-_cropped.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/YInMn_Blue" title="YInMn Blue">YInMn Blue</a> Inorganic blue pigment discovered in 2008 by <a href="/wiki/Mas_Subramanian" title="Mas Subramanian">Mas Subramanian</a> and Andrew E. Smith.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_natural_standards">Scientific natural standards</span></h3>
<ul><li>Emission spectrum of <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">Cu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span></li>
<li>Electronic spectrum of aqua-ions <span class="chemf nowrap">Cu(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Why_the_sky_and_sea_appear_blue">Why the sky and sea appear blue</span></h3>
<p>Of the colours in the visible spectrum of light, blue has a very short wavelength, while red has the longest wavelength. When sunlight passes through the atmosphere, the blue wavelengths are scattered more widely by the oxygen and nitrogen molecules, and more blue comes to our eyes. This effect is called <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a>, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Rayleigh" title="Lord Rayleigh">Lord Rayleigh</a>, the British physicist who discovered it. It was confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Near sunrise and sunset, most of the light we see comes in nearly tangent to the Earth's surface, so that the light's path through the atmosphere is so long that much of the blue and even green light is scattered out, leaving the sun rays and the clouds it illuminates red. Therefore, when looking at the sunset and sunrise, the colour red is more perceptible than any of the other colours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sea is seen as blue for largely the same reason: the water absorbs the longer wavelengths of red and reflects and scatters the blue, which comes to the eye of the viewer. The colour of the sea is also affected by the colour of the sky, reflected by particles in the water; and by <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and plant life in the water, which can make it look green; or by sediment, which can make it look brown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_perspective">Atmospheric perspective</span></h3>
<p>The farther away an object is, the more blue it often appears to the eye. For example, mountains in the distance often appear blue. This is the effect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_perspective" title="Atmospheric perspective">atmospheric perspective</a>; the farther an object is away from the viewer, the less contrast there is between the object and its background colour, which is usually blue. In a painting where different parts of the composition are blue, green and red, the blue will appear to be more distant, and the red closer to the viewer.  The cooler a colour is, the more distant it seems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 303.33333333333px"><div style="width: 303.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 301.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2010" data-file-width="3027" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/452px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/678px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/904px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg 2x" width="302"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Blue light is scattered more</a> than other wavelengths by the gases in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, giving the Earth a blue <a href="/wiki/Halo_(optical_phenomenon)" title="Halo (optical phenomenon)">halo</a> when seen from space.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_perspective_1.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Aerial_perspective_1.JPG/400px-Aerial_perspective_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Aerial_perspective_1.JPG/600px-Aerial_perspective_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Aerial_perspective_1.JPG/800px-Aerial_perspective_1.JPG 2x" width="267"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>An example of aerial, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_perspective" title="Atmospheric perspective">atmospheric perspective</a>. Objects become more blue and lighter in colour the farther they are from the viewer, because of <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="384" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg/240px-LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg/360px-LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/LightningVolt_Deep_Blue_Sea.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Under the sea, red and other light with longer wavelengths is absorbed, so white objects appear blue. The deeper you go, the darker the blue becomes. In the open sea, only about one per cent of light penetrates to a depth of 200 metres. (See <a href="/wiki/Underwater" title="Underwater">underwater</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphotic_depth" title="Euphotic depth">euphotic depth</a>)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blue_giant" title="Blue giant">Blue giants</a> are hot and luminous <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> with surface temperatures exceeding 10,000 K. The largest <a href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant_star" title="Blue supergiant star">blue supergiant stars</a> are extremely massive and energetic, and are usually unstable. They are generally short-lived, either exploding in a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> or periodically shedding their outer layers to become <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_eyes">Blue eyes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_eye_Stephen.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1900" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Blue_eye_Stephen.png/220px-Blue_eye_Stephen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Blue_eye_Stephen.png/330px-Blue_eye_Stephen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Blue_eye_Stephen.png/440px-Blue_eye_Stephen.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_eye_Stephen.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue eyes actually contain no blue pigment. The colour is caused by an effect called <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_effect" title="Tyndall effect">Tyndall scattering</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Blue eyes do not actually contain any blue pigment. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eye_colour" title="Eye colour">Eye colour</a> is determined by two factors: the <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigmentation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">eye</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wielgus_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wielgus-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prota_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prota-16">[16]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> of light by the <a href="/wiki/Turbidity" title="Turbidity">turbid</a> medium in the <a href="/wiki/Stroma_of_iris" title="Stroma of iris">stroma of the iris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-17">[17]</a></sup>  In humans, the pigmentation of the iris varies from light brown to black. The appearance of blue, green, and hazel eyes results from the <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_effect" title="Tyndall effect">Tyndall scattering</a> of light in the stroma, an optical effect similar to what accounts for the blueness of the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mason-18">[18]</a></sup> The irises of the eyes of people with blue eyes contain less dark <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> than those of people with brown eyes, which means that they absorb less short-wavelength blue light, which is instead reflected out to the viewer. Eye colour also varies depending on the lighting conditions, especially for lighter-coloured eyes.
</p><p>Blue eyes are most common in Ireland, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> area and Northern Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobe_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobe-19">[19]</a></sup> and are also found in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>. Blue eyes are also found in parts of Western Asia, most notably in Afghanistan, Syria, Iraq, and Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-altervista1_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-altervista1-20">[20]</a></sup>  In <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, 99% of people have blue eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-More_than_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-More_than-22">[22]</a></sup> In Denmark 30 years ago, only 8% of the population had brown eyes, though through immigration, today that number is about 11%. In Germany, about 75% have blue eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-More_than_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-More_than-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, as of 2006, one out of every six people, or 16.6% of the total population, and 22.3% of the <a href="/wiki/White_Americans" title="White Americans">white population</a>, have blue eyes, compared with about half of Americans born in 1900, and a third of Americans born in 1950. Blue eyes are becoming less common among American children. In the US, boys are 3–5 per cent more likely to have blue eyes than girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobe_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobe-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lasers">Lasers</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">Lasers</a> emitting in the blue region of the spectrum became widely available to the public in 2010 with the release of inexpensive high-powered 445-447 nm <a href="/wiki/Laser_diode" title="Laser diode">laser diode</a> technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laserglow_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-laserglow-23">[23]</a></sup> Previously the blue wavelengths were accessible only through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DPSS" title="DPSS">DPSS</a> which are comparatively expensive and inefficient, however these technologies are still widely used by the scientific community for applications including <a href="/wiki/Optogenetics" title="Optogenetics">optogenetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">Raman spectroscopy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Particle_image_velocimetry" title="Particle image velocimetry">particle image velocimetry</a>, due to their superior beam quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laserglow2_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-laserglow2-24">[24]</a></sup> Blue <a href="/wiki/Gas_laser" title="Gas laser">gas lasers</a> are also still commonly used for <a href="/wiki/Holography" title="Holography">holography</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequencing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optical_pumping" title="Optical pumping">optical pumping</a>, and other scientific and medical applications.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_ancient_world">In the ancient world</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2353" data-file-width="4117" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg/220px-Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg/330px-Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg/440px-Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ebih-Il_Louvre_AO17551_n11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up of the blue, lapis lazuli inlays used for the irises in the <i><a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Ebih-Il" title="Statue of Ebih-Il">Statue of Ebih-Il</a></i>, dating to the twenty-fifth century BC, discovered in the temple of <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Ishtar</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a></div></div></div>
<p>Blue was a latecomer among colours used in art and decoration, as well as language and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Reds, blacks, browns, and ochres are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_paintings" title="Cave paintings">cave paintings</a> from the Upper <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> period, but not blue. Blue was also not used for dyeing fabric until long after red, ochre, pink and purple. This is probably due to the perennial difficulty of making good blue dyes and pigments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The earliest known blue dyes were made from plants – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a> in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a> in Asia and Africa, while blue pigments were made from minerals, usually either <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> or <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>.
</p><p>Lapis lazuli, a semi-precious stone, has been mined in Afghanistan for more than three thousand years, and was exported to all parts of the ancient world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In Iran and Mesopotamia, it was used to make jewellery and vessels. In Egypt, it was used for the eyebrows on the funeral mask of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Tutankhamun" title="King Tutankhamun">King Tutankhamun</a> (1341–1323 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Importing lapis lazuli by caravan across the desert from Afghanistan to Egypt was very expensive. Beginning in about 2500 BC, the ancient Egyptians began to produce their own blue pigment known as <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_blue" title="Egyptian blue">Egyptian blue</a> by grinding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alkalai" title="Alkalai">alkalai</a>, and heating it to 800 or 900 °C (1,470 or 1,650 °F). This is considered the first synthetic pigment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-chase-29">[29]</a></sup> Egyptian blue was used to paint wood, papyrus and canvas, and was used to colour a glaze to make <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faience</a> beads, inlays, and pots. It was particularly used in funeral statuary and figurines and in tomb paintings. Blue was considered a beneficial colour which would protect the dead against evil in the afterlife. Blue dye was also used to colour the cloth in which mummies were wrapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Egypt blue was associated with the sky and with divinity. The Egyptian god <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> could make his skin blue so that he could fly, invisible, across the sky. Blue could also protect against evil; many people around the Mediterranean still wear a blue amulet, representing the eye of God, to protect them from misfortune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Blue glass was manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and Egypt as early as 2500 BC, using the same copper ingredients as Egyptian blue pigment. They also added cobalt, which produced a deeper blue, the same blue produced in the Middle Ages in the stained glass windows of the cathedrals of Saint-Denis and Chartres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ishtar_Gate" title="Ishtar Gate">Ishtar Gate</a> of ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> (604–562 BC) was decorated with deep blue glazed bricks used as a background for pictures of lions, dragons and <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Greeks classified colours by whether they were light or dark, rather than by their hue. The Greek word for dark blue, <i>kyaneos</i>, could also mean dark green, violet, black or brown. The ancient Greek word for a light blue, <i>glaukos</i>, also could mean light green, grey, or yellow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The Greeks imported indigo dye from India, calling it indikon. They used Egyptian blue in the wall paintings of <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>, in Crete, (2100 BC). It was not one of the four primary colours for Greek painting described by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> (red, yellow, black, and white), but nonetheless it was used as a background colour behind the friezes on Greek temples and to colour the beards of Greek statues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball106_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ball106-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Romans also imported indigo dye, but blue was the colour of working class clothing; the nobles and rich wore white, black, red or violet. Blue was considered the colour of mourning, and the colour of barbarians. Julius Caesar reported that the Celts and Germans dyed their faces blue to frighten their enemies, and tinted their hair blue when they grew old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Nonetheless, the Romans made extensive use of blue for decoration. According to <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>, they made dark blue pigment from indigo, and imported Egyptian blue pigment. The walls of Roman villas in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> had frescoes of brilliant blue skies, and blue pigments were found in the shops of colour merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball106_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ball106-35">[35]</a></sup> The Romans had many different words for varieties of blue, including <i>caeruleus</i>, <i>caesius</i>, <i>glaucus</i>, <i>cyaneus</i>, <i>lividus</i>, <i>venetus</i>, <i>aerius</i>, and <i>ferreus</i>, but two words, both of foreign origin, became the most enduring; <i>blavus</i>, from the Germanic word <i>blau</i>, which eventually became <i>bleu</i> or blue; and <i>azureus</i>, from the Arabic word <i>lazaward</i>, which became azure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Byzantine_Empire_and_the_Islamic_World">In the Byzantine Empire and the Islamic World</span></h3>
<p>Dark blue was widely used in the decoration of churches in the Byzantine Empire. In Byzantine art Christ and the Virgin Mary usually wore dark blue or purple. Blue was used as a background colour representing the sky in the magnificent mosaics which decorated Byzantine churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Islamic world, blue was of secondary importance to green, believed to be the favourite colour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophet_Mohammed" title="Prophet Mohammed">Prophet Mohammed</a>.  At certain times in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moorish_Spain" title="Moorish Spain">Moorish Spain</a> and other parts of the Islamic world, blue was the colour worn by Christians and Jews, because only Muslims were allowed to wear white and green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Dark blue and turquoise decorative tiles were widely used to decorate the facades and interiors of mosques and palaces from Spain to Central Asia.  Lapis lazuli pigment was also used to create the rich blues in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_miniatures" title="Persian miniatures">Persian miniatures</a>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 112.66666666667px"><div style="width: 112.66666666667px">

<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg/300px-4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg/451px-4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg/600px-4219_Istanbul_-_Topkapi_-_Harem_-_Sala_di_Murat_III_-_Foto_G._Dall%27Orto_27-5-2006.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155.33333333333px"><div style="width: 155.33333333333px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="During_the_Middle_Ages">During the Middle Ages</span></h3>
<p>In the art and life of Europe during the early Middle Ages, blue played a minor role. The nobility wore red or purple, while only the poor wore blue clothing, coloured with poor-quality dyes made from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a> plant. Blue played no part in the rich costumes of the clergy or the architecture or decoration of churches. This changed dramatically between 1130 and 1140 in Paris, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbe_Suger" title="Abbe Suger">Abbe Suger</a> rebuilt the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Denis_Basilica" title="Saint Denis Basilica">Saint Denis Basilica</a>. He installed <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> windows coloured with <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, which, combined with the light from the red glass, filled the church with a bluish violet light. The church became the marvel of the Christian world, and the colour became known as the "bleu de Saint-Denis". In the years that followed even more elegant blue stained glass windows were installed in other churches, including at <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a> in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another important factor in the increased prestige of the colour blue in the 12th century was the veneration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, and a change in the colours used to depict her clothing. In earlier centuries her robes had usually been painted in sombre black, grey, violet, dark green or dark blue. In the 12th century the Roma Catholic Church dictated that painters in Italy (and the rest of Europe consequently) to paint the Virgin Mary with the new most expensive pigment imported from Asia; <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Blue became associated with holiness, humility and virtue.
</p><p>Ultramarine was made from lapis lazuli, from the mines of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Badakshan" title="Badakshan">Badakshan</a>, in the mountains of Afghanistan, near the source of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus" title="Oxus">Oxus</a> River. The mines were visited by Marco Polo in about 1271; he reported, "here is found a high mountain from which they extract the finest and most beautiful of blues." Ground lapis was used in Byzantine manuscripts as early as the 6th century, but it was impure and varied greatly in colour. Ultramarine refined out the impurities through a long and difficult process, creating a rich and deep blue. It was called <i>bleu outremer</i> in French and <i>blu oltremare</i> in Italian, since it came from the other side of the sea. It cost far more than any other colour, and it became the luxury colour for the Kings and Princes of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>King <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a>, better known as Saint Louis (1214–1270), became the first king of France to regularly dress in blue. This was copied by other nobles.  Paintings of the mythical <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> began to show him dressed in blue.  The coat of arms of the kings of France became an azure or light blue shield, sprinkled with golden <a href="/wiki/Fleur-de-lis" title="Fleur-de-lis">fleur-de-lis</a> or lilies. Blue had come from obscurity to become the royal colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once blue became the colour of the king, it also became the colour of the wealthy and powerful in Europe. In the Middle Ages in France and to some extent in Italy, the dyeing of blue cloth was subject to license from the crown or state. In Italy, the dyeing of blue was assigned to a specific guild, the <i>tintori di guado,</i> and could not be done by anyone else without severe penalty. The wearing of blue implied some dignity and some wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides ultramarine, several other blues were widely used in the Middle Ages and later in the Renaissance. <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">Azurite</a>, a form of copper carbonate, was often used as a substitute for ultramarine. The Romans used it under the name lapis armenius, or Armenian stone. The British called it azure of Amayne, or German azure. The Germans themselves called it bergblau, or mountain stone. It was mined in France, Hungary, Spain and Germany, and it made a pale blue with a hint of green, which was ideal for painting skies. It was a favourite background colour of the German painter <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another blue often used in the Middle Ages was called tournesol or folium. It was made from the plant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crozophora_tinctoria" title="Crozophora tinctoria">Crozophora tinctoria</a>, which grew in the south of France. It made a fine transparent blue valued in medieval manuscripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another common blue pigment was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smalt" title="Smalt">smalt</a>, which was made by grinding blue cobalt glass into a fine powder. It made a deep violet blue similar to ultramarine, and was vivid in frescoes, but it lost some of its brilliance in oil paintings. It became especially popular in the 17th century, when ultramarine was difficult to obtain. It was employed at times by <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tintoretto" title="Tintoretto">Tintoretto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Veronese" title="Paolo Veronese">Veronese</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">El Greco</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Dyck" title="Van Dyck">Van Dyck</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubens" title="Rubens">Rubens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_European_Renaissance">In the European Renaissance</span></h3>
<p>In the Renaissance, a revolution occurred in painting; artists began to paint the world as it was actually seen, with perspective, depth, shadows, and light from a single source. Artists had to adapt their use of blue to the new rules.  In medieval paintings, blue was used to attract the attention of the viewer to the Virgin Mary, and identify her. In Renaissance paintings, artists tried to create harmonies between blue and red, lightening the blue with lead white paint and adding shadows and highlights. <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> was a master of this technique, carefully balancing the reds and the blues so no one colour dominated the picture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultramarine was the most prestigious blue of the Renaissance, and patrons sometimes specified that it be used in paintings they commissioned. The contract for the <i>Madone des Harpies</i> by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Sarto" title="Andrea del Sarto">Andrea del Sarto</a> (1514) required that the robe of the Virgin Mary be coloured with ultramarine costing "at least five good florins an ounce."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Good ultramarine was more expensive than gold; in 1508 the German painter <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a> reported in a letter that he had paid twelve ducats – the equivalent of forty-one grams of gold – for just thirty grams of ultramarine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Often painters or clients saved money by using less expensive blues, such as azurite smalt, or pigments made with indigo, but this sometimes caused problems. Pigments made from azurite were less expensive, but tended to turn dark and green with time. An example is the robe of the Virgin Mary in The <i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints_(Raphael)" title="Madonna and Child Enthroned with Saints (Raphael)">Madonna and Child Enthroned with Saints</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum" title="Metropolitan Museum">Metropolitan Museum</a> in New York. The Virgin Mary's azurite blue robe has degraded into a greenish-black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of oil painting changed the way colours looked and how they were used.  Ultramarine pigment, for instance, was much darker when used in oil painting than when used in <a href="/wiki/Tempera" title="Tempera">tempera</a> painting, in frescoes.  To balance their colours, Renaissance artists like Raphael added white to lighten the ultramarine.  The sombre dark blue robe of the Virgin Mary became a brilliant sky blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a> created his rich blues by using many thin glazes of paint of different blues and violets which allowed the light to pass through, which made a complex and luminous colour, like stained glass. He also used layers of finely ground or coarsely ground ultramarine, which gave subtle variations to the blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 150.66666666667px"><div style="width: 150.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 148.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="711" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg/223px-Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg/335px-Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg/446px-Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-18-_-_Adoration_of_the_Magi.jpg 2x" width="149"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> was one of the first Italian Renaissance painters to use <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, here in the murals of the Arena Chapel in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a> (circa 1305).
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 106px"><div style="width: 106px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 104px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angelico,_madonna_col_bambino,_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4722" data-file-width="3280" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/156px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/235px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg/312px-Angelico%2C_madonna_col_bambino%2C_pinacoteca_sabauda.jpg 2x" width="104"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Throughout the 14th and 15th centuries, the robes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> were painted with <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>. This is <i>The Virgin of Humility</i> by <a href="/wiki/Fra_Angelico" title="Fra Angelico">Fra Angelico</a> (about 1430). Blue fills the picture.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 118.66666666667px"><div style="width: 118.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 116.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raffael_030.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="998" data-file-width="776" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Raffael_030.jpg/175px-Raffael_030.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Raffael_030.jpg/263px-Raffael_030.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Raffael_030.jpg/350px-Raffael_030.jpg 2x" width="117"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_del_Prato_(Raphael)" title="Madonna del Prato (Raphael)">Madonna of the Meadow</a></i> (1506), Raphael used white to soften the ultramarine blue of Virgin Mary's robes to balance the red and blue, and to harmonise with the rest of the picture.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 168px"><div style="width: 168px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 166px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4226" data-file-width="4670" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/249px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/374px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/497px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg 2x" width="166"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a> used an ultramarine sky and robes to give depth and brilliance to his <i><a href="/wiki/Bacchus_and_Ariadne" title="Bacchus and Ariadne">Bacchus and Ariadne</a></i> (1520–1523)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 118px"><div style="width: 118px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 116px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3722" data-file-width="2880" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg/174px-Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg/261px-Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg/348px-Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints.jpg 2x" width="116"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>In this painting of <i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_and_Child_Enthroned_with_Saints_(Raphael)" title="Madonna and Child Enthroned with Saints (Raphael)">The Madonna and Child Enthroned with Saints</a></i> an early work by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, the blue cloak of the Virgin Mary has turned a green-black. It was painted with less-expensive <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 136px"><div style="width: 136px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 134px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_(2).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1610" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg/201px-Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg/302px-Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg/402px-Andrea_della_Robbia_-_Virgin_Adoring_the_Christ_Child_-_Walters_27212_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="134"/></a></div></div>
