</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 98.666666666667px"><div style="width: 98.666666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 96.666666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2062" data-file-width="1333" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg/145px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg/218px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg/291px-Les_Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_duc_de_Berry_Janvier.jpg 2x" width="97"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A8s_Riches_Heures_du_Duc_de_Berry" title="Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry">Très Riches Heures du Duc de Berry</a> was the most important illuminated manuscript of the 15th century. The blue was the extravagantly expensive ultramarine.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_and_white_porcelain">Blue and white porcelain</span></h3>
<p>In about the 9th century, Chinese artisans abandoned the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_blue" title="Han blue">Han blue</a> colour they had used for centuries, and began to use <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a>, made with <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> salts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a>, to manufacture fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white porcelain</a>, The plates and vases were shaped, dried, the paint applied with a brush, covered with a clear glaze, then fired at a high temperature. Beginning in the 14th century, this type of porcelain was exported in large quantity to Europe where it inspired a whole style of art, called <a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">Chinoiserie</a>.  European courts tried for many years to imitate Chinese blue and white porcelain, but only succeeded in the 18th century after a missionary brought the secret back from China.
</p><p>Other famous white and blue patterns appeared in Delft, Meissen, Staffordshire, and Saint Petersburg, Russia.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 163.33333333333px"><div style="width: 163.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 161.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2735" data-file-width="2206" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg/242px-Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg/363px-Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg/484px-Early_blue_and_white_ware_circa_1335_Jingdezhen.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">blue and white porcelain</a> from about 1335, made in Jingdezhen, the porcelain centre of China. Exported to Europe, this porcelain launched the style of <a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">Chinoiserie</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120.66666666667px"><div style="width: 120.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 118.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2426" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg/178px-Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg/267px-Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg/356px-Rouen_porcelain_vase_end_of_the_17th_century.jpg 2x" width="119"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Soft-paste_porcelain" title="Soft-paste porcelain">soft-paste porcelain</a> vase made in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>, France, at the end of the 17th century, imitating Chinese blue and white.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 172px"><div style="width: 172px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 170px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1357" data-file-width="1152" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg/255px-Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg/382px-Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg/509px-Delftware_pushkin_museum01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></div></div>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg/400px-LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg/600px-LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/33/LFZ_chinaware_3.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a></div></div>

<h3><span id="War_of_the_blues_.E2.80.93_indigo_versus_woad"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_the_blues_–_indigo_versus_woad">War of the blues – indigo versus woad</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4794" data-file-width="4095" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg/220px-Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg/330px-Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg/440px-Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meisje_met_de_parel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Vermeer" title="Johannes Vermeer">Johannes Vermeer</a> used natural ultramarine in his paintings, as in his <i><a href="/wiki/Girl_with_a_Pearl_Earring" title="Girl with a Pearl Earring">Girl with a Pearl Earring</a></i>. The expense was probably borne by his wealthy patron <a href="/wiki/Pieter_van_Ruijven" title="Pieter van Ruijven">Pieter van Ruijven</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>While blue was an expensive and prestigious colour in European painting, it became a common colour for clothing during the Renaissance. The rise of the colour blue in fashion in the 12th and 13th centuries led to a blue dye industry in several cities, notably <a href="/wiki/Amiens" title="Amiens">Amiens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Erfurt" title="Erfurt">Erfurt</a>. They made a dye called pastel from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a>, a plant common in Europe, which had been used to make blue dye by the Celts and German tribes.  Blue became a colour worn by domestics and artisans, not just nobles. In 1570, when Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pius_V" title="Pius V">Pius V</a> listed the colours that could be used for ecclesiastical dress and for altar decoration, he excluded blue, because he considered it too common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The process of making blue with woad was long and noxious – it involved soaking the leaves of the plant for from three days to a week in human urine, ideally urine from men who had been drinking a great deal of alcohol, which was said to improve the colour. The fabric was then soaked for a day in the resulting mixture, then put out in the sun, where as it dried it turned blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pastel industry was threatened in the 15th century by the arrival from India of the same dye (<a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a>), obtained from a shrub widely grown in Asia. The Asian indigo dye precursors is more readily obtained. In 1498, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasco_de_Gama" title="Vasco de Gama">Vasco de Gama</a> opened a trade route to import indigo from India to Europe.  In India, the indigo leaves were soaked in water, fermented, pressed into cakes, dried into bricks, then carried to the ports London, Marseille, Genoa, and Bruges. Later, in the 17th century, the British, Spanish, and Dutch established indigo plantations in Jamaica, South Carolina, the Virgin Islands and South America, and began to import American indigo to Europe.
</p><p>The countries with large and prosperous pastel industries tried to block the use of indigo. The German government outlawed the use of indigo in 1577, describing it as a "pernicious, deceitful and corrosive substance, the Devil's dye."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In France, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a>, in an edict of 1609, forbade under pain of death the use of "the false and pernicious Indian drug".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> It was forbidden in England until 1611, when British traders established their own indigo industry in India and began to import it into Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The efforts to block indigo were in vain; the quality of indigo blue was too high and the price too low for pastel made from woad to compete. In 1737 both the French and German governments finally allowed the use of indigo. This ruined the dye industries in Toulouse and the other cities that produced pastel, but created a thriving new indigo commerce to seaports such as Bordeaux, Nantes and Marseille.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another war of the blues took place at the end of the 19th century, between indigo and synthetic indigo, discovered in 1868 by the German chemist Johann Friedrich Wilhelm Adolf von Baeyer. The German chemical firm BASF put the new dye on the market in 1897, in direct competition with the British-run indigo industry in India, which produced most of the world's indigo. In 1897 Britain sold ten thousand tons of natural indigo on the world market, while BASF sold six hundred tons of synthetic indigo. The British industry cut prices and reduced the salaries of its workers, but it was unable to compete; the synthetic indigo was more pure, made a more lasting blue, and was not dependent upon good or bad harvests. In 1911, India sold only 660 tons of natural indigo, while BASF sold 22,000 tons of synthetic indigo.  In 2002, more than 38,000 tons of synthetic indigo was produced, often for the production of blue jeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG/300px-Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG/451px-Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG/600px-Isatis_tinctoria02.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Isatis_tinctoria" title="Isatis tinctoria">Isatis tinctoria</a>, or woad, was the main source of blue dye in Europe from ancient times until the arrival of indigo from Asia and America. It was processed into a paste called pastel.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125.33333333333px"><div style="width: 125.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 123.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="638" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG/185px-Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG/277px-Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG/369px-Schreber_woad_mill_1752.JPG 2x" width="124"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A woad mill in <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a>, in Germany, in 1752. The woad industry was already on its way to extinction, unable to compete with indigo blue.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 156px"><div style="width: 156px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 154px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3658" data-file-width="3755" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg/231px-The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg/347px-The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg/462px-The_Hunt_of_the_Unicorn_Tapestry_1.jpg 2x" width="154"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A Dutch tapestry from 1495 to 1505. The blue colour comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woad" title="Woad">woad</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg/300px-Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg/451px-Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg/600px-Indigofera_tinctoria1.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">Indigofera tinctoria</a></i>, a tropical shrub, is the main source of indigo dye. The chemical composition of indigo dye is the same as that of woad, but the colour is more intense.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 334px"><div style="width: 334px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 332px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1505" data-file-width="3329" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg/498px-Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg/748px-Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg/996px-Indigo-Historische_Farbstoffsammlung.jpg 2x" width="332"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Cakes of indigo. The leaf has been soaked in water, fermented, mixed with lye or another base, then pressed into cakes and dried, ready for export.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_uniform">Blue uniform</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg/220px-5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg/330px-5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg/440px-5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5.29.10NYPDByLuigiNovi6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>New York City police officers on Times Square (2010).</div></div></div>
<p>In the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William,_Elector_of_Brandenburg" title="Frederick William, Elector of Brandenburg">Frederick William, Elector of Brandenburg</a>, was one of the first rulers to give his army blue uniforms. The reasons were economic; the German states were trying to protect their pastel dye industry against competition from imported indigo dye. When Brandenburg became the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> in 1701, the uniform colour was adopted by the Prussian army. Most German soldiers wore dark blue uniforms until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, with the exception of the Bavarians, who wore light blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thanks in part to the availability of indigo dye, the 18th century saw the widespread use of blue military uniforms. Prior to 1748, British naval officers simply wore upper-class civilian clothing and wigs. In 1748, the British uniform for naval officers was officially established as an embroidered coat of the colour then called marine blue, now known as <a href="/wiki/Navy_blue" title="Navy blue">navy blue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Navy" title="Continental Navy">Continental Navy</a> of the United States was created in 1775, it largely copied the British uniform and colour.
</p><p>In the late 18th century, the blue uniform became a symbol of liberty and revolution. In October 1774, even before the United States declared its independence, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a> and one hundred Virginia neighbours of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> organised a voluntary militia unit (the Fairfax County Independent Company of Volunteers) and elected Washington the honorary commander.  For their uniforms they chose blue and <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buff</a>, the colours of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig Party</a>, the opposition party in England, whose policies were supported by George Washington and many other <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">patriots</a> in the American colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> was established in 1775 at the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> declared that the official uniform colour would be brown, but this was not popular with many militias, whose officers were already wearing blue. In 1778 the Congress asked George Washington to design a new uniform, and in 1779 Washington made the official colour of all uniforms blue and <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buff</a>. Blue continued to be the colour of the field uniform of the US Army until 1902, and is still the colour of the dress uniform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In France the <a href="/wiki/Gardes_Fran%C3%A7aises" title="Gardes Françaises">Gardes Françaises</a>, the elite regiment which protected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a>, wore dark blue uniforms with red trim. In 1789, the soldiers gradually changed their allegiance from the king to the people, and they played a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">storming of the Bastille</a>. After the fall of Bastille, a new armed force, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garde_Nationale" title="Garde Nationale">Garde Nationale</a>, was formed under the command of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a>, who had served with George Washington in America. Lafayette gave the Garde Nationale dark blue uniforms similar to those of the Continental Army. Blue became the colour of the revolutionary armies, opposed to the white uniforms of the Royalists and the Austrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> abandoned many of the doctrines of the French Revolution but he kept blue as the uniform colour for his army, although he had great difficulty obtaining the blue dye, since the British controlled the seas and blocked the importation of indigo to France. Napoleon was forced to dye uniforms with woad, which had an inferior blue colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The French army wore a dark blue uniform coat with red trousers until 1915, when it was found to be a too visible target on the battlefields of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.  It was replaced with uniforms of a light blue-grey colour called horizon blue.
</p><p>Blue was the colour of liberty and revolution in the 18th century, but in the 19th it increasingly became the colour of government authority, the uniform colour of policemen and other public servants. It was considered serious and authoritative, without being menacing. In 1829, when <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> created the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Metropolitan_Police" title="London Metropolitan Police">London Metropolitan Police</a>, he made the colour of the uniform jacket a dark, almost black blue, to make the policemen look different from soldiers, who until then had patrolled the streets. The traditional blue jacket with silver buttons of the London "bobbie" was not abandoned until the mid-1990s, when it was replaced by a light blue shirt and a jumper or sweater of the colour officially known as NATO blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police_Department" title="New York City Police Department">New York City Police Department</a>, modelled after the London Metropolitan Police, was created in 1844, and in 1853, they were officially given a navy blue uniform, the colour they wear today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-okidegbe_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-okidegbe-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Navy blue is one of the most popular school uniform colours, with the Toronto Catholic District School Board adopting a dress code policy which requires students system-wide to wear white tops and navy blue bottoms.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_the_perfect_blue">Search for the perfect blue</span></h3>
<p>During the 17th and 18th centuries, chemists in Europe tried to discover a way to create synthetic blue pigments, avoiding the expense of importing and grinding lapis lazuli, azurite and other minerals. The Egyptians had created a synthetic colour, Egyptian blue, three thousand years BC, but the formula had been lost. The Chinese had also created synthetic pigments, but the formula was not known in the west.
</p><p>In 1709 a German druggist and pigment maker named <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jacob_Diesbach" title="Johann Jacob Diesbach">Johann Jacob Diesbach</a> accidentally discovered a new blue while experimenting with potassium and iron sulphides. The new colour was first called Berlin blue, but later became known as <a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a>. By 1710 it was being used by the French painter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antoine_Watteau" title="Antoine Watteau">Antoine Watteau</a>, and later his successor <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Lancret" title="Nicolas Lancret">Nicolas Lancret</a>. It became immensely popular for the manufacture of wallpaper, and in the 19th century was widely used by French impressionist painters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the 1820s, Prussian blue was imported into Japan through the port of <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>. It was called <i>bero-ai</i>, or Berlin blue, and it became popular because it did not fade like traditional Japanese blue pigment, <i>ai-gami</i>, made from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dayflower" title="Dayflower">dayflower</a>. Prussian blue was used by both <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a>, in his famous wave paintings, and <a href="/wiki/Hiroshige" title="Hiroshige">Hiroshige</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1824 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Societ%C3%A9_pour_l%27Encouragement_d%27Industrie" title="Societé pour l'Encouragement d'Industrie">Societé pour l'Encouragement d'Industrie</a> in France offered a prize for the invention of an artificial <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a> which could rival the natural colour made from lapis lazuli. The prize was won in 1826 by a chemist named Jean Baptiste Guimet, but he refused to reveal the formula of his colour. In 1828, another scientist, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gmelin" title="Christian Gmelin">Christian Gmelin</a> then a professor of chemistry in Tübingen, found the process and published his formula. This was the beginning of new industry to manufacture artificial ultramarine, which eventually almost completely replaced the natural product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1878 a German chemist named a. Von Baeyer discovered a synthetic substitute for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigotine" title="Indigotine">indigotine</a>, the active ingredient of indigo. This product gradually replaced natural indigo, and after the end of the First World War, it brought an end to the trade of indigo from the East and West Indies.
</p><p>In 1901 a new synthetic blue dye, called <a href="/wiki/Indanthrone_blue" title="Indanthrone blue">Indanthrone blue</a>, was invented, which had even greater resistance to fading during washing or in the sun. This dye gradually replaced artificial indigo, whose production ceased in about 1970. Today almost all blue clothing is dyed with an indanthrone blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 137.33333333333px"><div style="width: 137.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 135.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Blue_Boy.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1289" data-file-width="872" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Blue_Boy.jpg/203px-The_Blue_Boy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Blue_Boy.jpg/304px-The_Blue_Boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/The_Blue_Boy.jpg/406px-The_Blue_Boy.jpg 2x" width="136"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gainsborough" title="Thomas Gainsborough">Thomas Gainsborough</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Boy" title="The Blue Boy">The Blue Boy</a></i> includes "the lavish lapis lazuli, the darker indigo pigment and the paler cobalt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 294.66666666667px"><div style="width: 294.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 292.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5640" data-file-width="8242" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg/439px-Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg/658px-Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg/877px-Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa2.jpg 2x" width="293"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 286.66666666667px"><div style="width: 286.66666666667px">

</p>

</div></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impressionist_painters">Impressionist painters</span></h3>
<p>The invention of new synthetic pigments in the 18th and 19th centuries considerably brightened and expanded the palette of painters. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.M.W._Turner" title="J.M.W. Turner">J.M.W. Turner</a> experimented with the new cobalt blue, and of the twenty colours most used by the <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">Impressionists</a>, twelve were new and synthetic colours, including cobalt blue, ultramarine and cerulean blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball265_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ball265-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another important influence on painting in the 19th century was the theory of complementary colours, developed by the French chemist Michel Eugene Chevreul in 1828 and published in 1839. He demonstrated that placing complementary colours, such as blue and yellow-orange or ultramarine and yellow, next to each other heightened the intensity of each colour "to the apogee of their tonality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>  In 1879 an American physicist, Ogden Rood, published a book charting the complementary colours of each colour in the spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This principle of painting was used by Claude Monet in his <i>Impression – Sunrise – Fog</i> (1872), where he put a vivid blue next to a bright orange sun, (1872) and in <i>Régate à Argenteuil</i> (1872), where he painted an orange sun against blue water. The colours brighten each other. Renoir used the same contrast of cobalt blue water and an orange sun in <i>Canotage sur la Seine</i> (1879–1880). Both Monet and Renoir liked to use pure colours, without any blending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ball265_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ball265-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monet and the impressionists were among the first to observe that shadows were full of colour. In his <i>La Gare Saint-Lazare</i>, the grey smoke, vapour and dark shadows are actually composed of mixtures of bright pigment, including cobalt blue, cerulean blue, synthetic ultramarine, emerald green, Guillet green, chrome yellow, vermilion and ecarlate red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Blue was a favourite colour of the impressionist painters, who used it not just to depict nature but to create moods, feelings and atmospheres. <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">Cobalt blue</a>, a pigment of cobalt oxide-aluminium oxide, was a favourite of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auguste_Renoir" title="Auguste Renoir">Auguste Renoir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>. It was similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smalt" title="Smalt">smalt</a>, a pigment used for centuries to make blue glass, but it was much improved by the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Louis Jacques Thénard</a>, who introduced it in 1802.  It was very stable but extremely expensive. Van Gogh wrote to his brother Theo, "'Cobalt [blue] is a divine colour and there is nothing so beautiful for putting atmosphere around things ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Van Gogh described to his brother Theo how he composed a sky: "The dark blue sky is spotted with clouds of an even darker blue than the fundamental blue of intense cobalt, and others of a lighter blue, like the bluish white of the Milky Way ... the sea was very dark ultramarine, the shore a sort of violet and of light red as I see it, and on the dunes, a few bushes of prussian blue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 248px"><div style="width: 248px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 246px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2637" data-file-width="3606" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg/369px-La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg/554px-La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg/739px-La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg 2x" width="246"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Claude Monet</a> used several recently invented colours in his <i>Gare Saint-Lazare</i> (1877). He used <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a>, invented in 1807, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerulean_blue" title="Cerulean blue">cerulean blue</a> invented in 1860, and French <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, first made in 1828.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 280.66666666667px"><div style="width: 280.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 278.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Claude_Monet_042.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Claude_Monet_042.jpg/418px-Claude_Monet_042.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Claude_Monet_042.jpg/627px-Claude_Monet_042.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Claude_Monet_042.jpg/836px-Claude_Monet_042.jpg 2x" width="279"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>In <i>Régate à Argenteuil</i> (1872), Monet used two complementary colours together — blue and orange — to brighten the effect of both colours.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 116px"><div style="width: 116px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 114px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Auguste_Renoir,_The_Umbrellas,_ca._1881-86.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4226" data-file-width="2687" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg/171px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg/257px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg/343px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_The_Umbrellas%2C_ca._1881-86.jpg 2x" width="114"/></a></div></div>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 224px"><div style="width: 224px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i>Wheatfield Under Thunderclouds</i> (July 1890), one of the last paintings by <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>. He wrote of cobalt blue, "there is nothing so beautiful for putting atmosphere around things."
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_suit">Blue suit</span></h3>
<p>Blue had first become the high fashion colour of the wealthy and powerful in Europe in the 13th century, when it was worn by <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a>, better known as Saint Louis (1214-1270).  Wearing blue implied dignity and wealth, and blue clothing was restricted to the nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> However, blue was replaced by black as the power colour in the 14th century, when European princes, and then merchants and bankers, wanted to show their seriousness, dignity and devoutness (see <a href="/wiki/Black" title="Black">Black</a>).
</p><p>Blue gradually returned to court fashion in the 17th century, as part of a palette of peacock-bright colours shown off in extremely elaborate costumes. The modern blue business suit has its roots in England in the middle of the 17th century. Following the London plague of 1665 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_fire" title="London fire">London fire</a> of 1666, King <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II of England</a> ordered that his courtiers wear simple coats, waistcoats and breeches, and the palette of colours became blue, grey, white and buff. Widely imitated, this style of men's fashion became almost a uniform of the London merchant class and the English country gentleman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuitablyDressed_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-SuitablyDressed-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the American Revolution, the leader of the Whig Party in England, <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_Fox" title="Charles James Fox">Charles James Fox</a>, wore a blue coat and buff waistcoat and breeches, the colours of the Whig Party and of the uniform of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, whose principles he supported. The men's suit followed the basic form of the military uniforms of the time, particularly the uniforms of the cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuitablyDressed_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-SuitablyDressed-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 19th century, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Regency" title="British Regency">Regency</a> of the future King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_IV" title="George IV">George IV</a>, the blue suit was revolutionised by a courtier named George <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beau_Brummel" title="Beau Brummel">Beau Brummel</a>. Brummel created a suit that closely fitted the human form.  The new style had a long tail coat cut to fit the body and long tight trousers to replace the knee-length breeches and stockings of the previous century. He used plain colours, such as blue and grey, to concentrate attention on the form of the body, not the clothes. Brummel observed, "If people turn to look at you in the street, you are not well dressed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>  This fashion was adopted by the Prince Regent, then by London society and the upper classes.  Originally the coat and trousers were different colours, but in the 19th century the suit of a single colour became fashionable. By the late 19th century the black suit had become the uniform of businessmen in England and America. In the 20th century, the black suit was largely replaced by the dark blue or grey suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuitablyDressed_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-SuitablyDressed-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124px"><div style="width: 124px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 122px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis-innocentiv.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="344" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Louis-innocentiv.jpg/183px-Louis-innocentiv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Louis-innocentiv.jpg/275px-Louis-innocentiv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/df/Louis-innocentiv.jpg 2x" width="122"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>King <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a> (on the right, with Pope Innocent) was the first European king to wear blue. It quickly became the colour of the nobles and wealthy.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 100.66666666667px"><div style="width: 100.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 98.666666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson,_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="422" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg/148px-Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg/223px-Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg/297px-Anthony_Lee_Portrait_of_Joseph_Leeson%2C_later_1st_Earl_of_Milltown.jpg 2x" width="99"/></a></div></div>

</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 134px"><div style="width: 134px">

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 71.333333333333px"><div style="width: 71.333333333333px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 303.33333333333px"><div style="width: 303.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_20th_and_21st_century">In the 20th and 21st century</span></h3>
<p>At the beginning of the 20th century, many artists recognised the emotional power of blue, and made it the central element of paintings. During his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Period" title="Blue Period">Blue Period</a> (1901–1904) <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> used blue and green, with hardly any warm colours, to create a melancholy mood. In Russia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbolist" title="Symbolist">symbolist</a> painter <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Kuznetsov" title="Pavel Kuznetsov">Pavel Kuznetsov</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Rose_(art_group)" title="Blue Rose (art group)">Blue Rose</a> art group (1906–1908) used blue to create a fantastic and exotic atmosphere. In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">Wassily Kandinsky</a> and other Russian émigrés formed the art group called <a href="/wiki/Der_Blaue_Reiter" title="Der Blaue Reiter">Der Blaue Reiter</a> (The Blue Rider), and used blue to symbolise spirituality and eternity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a> used intense blues to express the emotions he wanted viewers to feel. Matisse wrote, "A certain blue penetrates your soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the art of the second half of the 20th century, painters of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_expressionist" title="Abstract expressionist">abstract expressionist</a> movement began to use blue and other colours in pure form, without any attempt to represent anything, to inspire ideas and emotions. Painter <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rothko" title="Mark Rothko">Mark Rothko</a> observed that colour was "only an instrument;" his interest was "in expressing human emotions tragedy, ecstasy, doom, and so on."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In fashion blue, particularly dark blue, was seen as a colour which was serious but not grim. In the mid-20th century, blue passed black as the most common colour of men's business suits, the costume usually worn by political and business leaders. Public opinion polls in the United States and Europe showed that blue was the favourite colour of over fifty per cent of respondents. Green was far behind with twenty per cent, while white and red received about eight per cent each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1873 a German immigrant in San Francisco, <a href="/wiki/Levi_Strauss" title="Levi Strauss">Levi Strauss</a>, invented a sturdy kind of work trousers, made of <a href="/wiki/Denim" title="Denim">denim</a> fabric and coloured with <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a> dye, called <a href="/wiki/Jeans" title="Jeans">blue jeans</a>. In 1935, they were raised to the level of high fashion by <a href="/wiki/Vogue_(magazine)" title="Vogue (magazine)">Vogue magazine</a>. Beginning in the 1950s, they became an essential part of uniform of young people in the United States, Europe, and around the world.
</p><p>Blue was also seen as a colour which was authoritative without being threatening. Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, blue was adopted as the colour of important international organisations, including the United Nations, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, the European Union, and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>. United Nations peacekeepers wear blue helmets to stress their peacekeeping role. Blue is used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NATO_Military_Symbols_for_Land_Based_Systems" title="NATO Military Symbols for Land Based Systems">NATO Military Symbols for Land Based Systems</a> to denote friendly forces, hence the term "blue on blue" for <a href="/wiki/Friendly_fire" title="Friendly fire">friendly fire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blue_Force_Tracking" title="Blue Force Tracking">Blue Force Tracking</a> for location of friendly units. The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> of China (formerly known as the "Red Army") uses the term "Blue Army" to refer to hostile forces during exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 20th century saw the invention of new ways of creating blue, such as <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a>, making blue light through a chemical reaction.
</p><p>In the 20th century, it also became possible to own your own colour of blue. The French artist <a href="/wiki/Yves_Klein" title="Yves Klein">Yves Klein</a>, with the help of a French paint dealer, created a specific blue called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Klein_blue" title="International Klein blue">International Klein blue</a>, which he patented. It was made of ultramarine combined with a resin called Rhodopa, which gave it a particularly brilliant colour. The baseball team the Los Angeles Dodgers developed its own blue, called <a href="/wiki/Dodger_blue" title="Dodger blue">Dodger blue</a>, and several American universities invented new blues for their colours.
</p><p>With the dawn of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>, blue has become the standard colour for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperlinks" title="Hyperlinks">hyperlinks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphic browsers</a> (though in most browsers links turn purple if you visit their target), to make their presence within text obvious to readers.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 122px"><div style="width: 122px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 120px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg/180px-Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg/270px-Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg/360px-Old_guitarist_chicago.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>During his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Period" title="Blue Period">Blue Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> used blue as the colour of melancholy, as in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Guitarist" title="The Old Guitarist">The Old Guitarist</a></i>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 194.66666666667px"><div style="width: 194.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 192.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg/289px-Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg/433px-Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg/578px-Kuznetsov_vstepi.jpg 2x" width="193"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 189.33333333333px"><div style="width: 189.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i>The Blue Rider</i> (1903), by <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">Wassily Kandinsky</a>, For Kandinsky, blue was the colour of spirituality: the darker the blue, the more it awakened human desire for the eternal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 224.66666666667px"><div style="width: 224.66666666667px">

<p><i>The Conversation</i> (1908–1912) by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>  used blue to express the emotions he wanted the viewer to feel.
</p>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 138.66666666667px"><div style="width: 138.66666666667px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Vivid blues can be created by chemical reactions, called <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a>. This is <a href="/wiki/Luminol" title="Luminol">luminol</a>, a chemical used in crime scene investigations. Luminol glows blue when it contacts even a tiny trace of blood.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_world_culture">In world culture</span></h2>
<ul><li>In the English language, blue often represents the human emotion of sadness, for example, "He was feeling blue".</li>
<li>In German, to be "blue" (<i>blau sein</i>) is to be drunk. This derives from the ancient use of urine, particularly the urine of men who had been drinking alcohol in dyeing cloth blue with woad or indigo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> It may also be in relation to rain, which is usually regarded as a trigger of depressive emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>Blue can sometimes represent happiness and optimism in popular songs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> usually referring to blue skies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the German, Swedish and Norwegian languages, a naive person is said to look upon the world with a blue eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>Blue is commonly used in the Western Hemisphere to symbolise boys, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Pink" title="Pink">pink</a> used for girls. In the early 1900s, blue was the colour for girls, since it had traditionally been the colour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Art" title="Western Art">Western Art</a>, while pink was for boys (as it was akin to the colour <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a>, considered a masculine colour).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>In China, the colour blue is commonly associated with torment, ghosts, and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-96">[96]</a></sup>  In a traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">Chinese opera</a>, a character with a face powdered blue is a villain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Turkey and Central Asia, blue is the colour of mourning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>The men of the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg people</a> in North Africa wear a blue turban called a <a href="/wiki/Tagelmust" title="Tagelmust">tagelmust</a>, which protects them from the sun and wind-blown sand of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_desert" title="Sahara desert">Sahara desert</a>. It is coloured with indigo. Instead of using dye, which uses precious water, the tagelmust is coloured by pounding it with powdered indigo. The blue colour transfers to the skin, where it is seen as a sign of nobility and affluence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBP_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBP-98">[98]</a></sup> Early visitors called them the "Blue Men" of the Sahara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the culture of the <a href="/wiki/Hopi" title="Hopi">Hopi</a> people of the American southwest, blue symbolised the west, which was seen as the house of death. A dream about a person carrying a blue feather was considered a very bad omen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Thailand, blue is associated with Friday on the <a href="/wiki/Thai_solar_calendar" title="Thai solar calendar">Thai solar calendar</a>. Anyone may wear blue on Fridays and anyone born on a Friday may adopt blue as their colour.</li></ul>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tuareg2.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1537" data-file-width="1153" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tuareg2.JPG/120px-Tuareg2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tuareg2.JPG/180px-Tuareg2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Tuareg2.JPG/240px-Tuareg2.JPG 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A man of the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg people</a> of North Africa wears a <a href="/wiki/Tagelmust" title="Tagelmust">tagelmust</a> or turban dyed with indigo. The indigo stains their skin blue; they were known by early visitors as "the blue men" of the desert.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_national_and_international_colour">As a national and international colour</span></h3>
<p>Various shades of blue are used as the <a href="/wiki/National_colours" title="National colours">national colours</a> for many nations.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Azure_(color)" title="Azure (color)">Azure</a>, a light blue, is the national colour of Italy (from the <a href="/wiki/Livery" title="Livery">livery</a> colour of the former reigning family, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Savoy" title="House of Savoy">House of Savoy</a>). National sport clubs are known as the <i>Azzurri</i>.</li>
<li>Blue and white are the national colours of Scotland, Argentina, El Salvador, Finland, Greece, Guatemala, Honduras, Israel, <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Micronesia</a>, Nicaragua and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, are the ancient national colours of Portugal and are the colours of the United Nations.</li>
<li>Blue, white and yellow are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> and Uruguay.</li>
<li>Blue, white and green are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>.</li>
<li>Blue, white and black are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li>Blue and yellow are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>, Sweden, and Ukraine.</li>
<li>Blue, yellow and green are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>.</li>
<li>Blue, yellow and red are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, Colombia, Ecuador, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, and Venezuela.</li>
<li>Blue and red are the national colours of <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>.</li>
<li>Blue, red and white are the national colours of Australia, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, Chile, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, Cuba, the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, France, Iceland, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, the Netherlands, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, Norway, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, the United Kingdom, and the United States.</li>
<li>Blue, called <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick%27s_blue" title="St. Patrick's blue">St. Patrick's blue</a>, is a traditional colour of Ireland, and appears on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arms_of_Ireland" title="Arms of Ireland">Arms of Ireland</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_colour" title="Political colour">Political colour</a></div>
<ul><li>In the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, the Blues and the Greens were the most prominent political factions in the capital. They took their names from the colours of the two most popular <a href="/wiki/Chariot_racing" title="Chariot racing">chariot racing</a> teams at the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_of_Constantinople" title="Hippodrome of Constantinople">Hippodrome of Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edward_Gibbon_1960_pg._554_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_Gibbon_1960_pg._554-102">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>The word <i>blue</i> was used in England the 17th century as a disparaging reference to rigid moral codes and those who observed them, particularly in <i>blue-stocking</i>, a reference to Oliver Cromwell's supporters in the parliament of 1653.</li>
<li>In the middle of the 18th century, blue was the colour of <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> party, then the opposition party in England, Scotland and Ireland, which supported the British monarch and power of the landed aristocracy, while the ruling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" title="Whig (British political party)">Whigs</a> had orange as their colour.  Flags of the two colours are seen over a polling station in the series of prints by <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humours_of_an_election" title="Humours of an election">Humours of an election</a>,  made in 1754–55. Blue remains the colour of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party of the UK</a> today.</li>
<li>By the time of the American Revolution,  The Tories were in power and blue and buff had become the colours of the opposition <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" title="Whig (British political party)">Whigs</a>. They were the subject of a famous toast to Whig politicians by Mrs. Crewe in 1784; "Buff and blue and all of you." They also became the colours of the American patriots in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, who had strong Whig sympathies, and of the uniforms of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> led by <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/War_in_the_Vend%C3%A9e" title="War in the Vendée">revolt in the Vendée</a> that followed, blue was the colour worn by the soldiers of the Revolutionary government, while the royalists wore white.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breton_blues" title="Breton blues">Breton blues</a> were members of a liberal, anti-clerical political movement in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> in the late 19th century.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Blueshirts" title="Blueshirts">blueshirts</a> were members of an extreme right paramilitary organisation active in Ireland during the 1930s.</li>
<li>Blue is associated with numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre-right" title="Centre-right">centre-right</a> <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> political parties in Europe, including the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_for_Freedom_and_Democracy" title="People's Party for Freedom and Democracy">People's Party for Freedom and Democracy</a> (Netherlands), the <a href="/wiki/Mouvement_R%C3%A9formateur" title="Mouvement Réformateur">Reformist Movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Open_Vlaamse_Liberalen_en_Democraten" title="Open Vlaamse Liberalen en Democraten">Open VLD</a> (Belgium), the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(Luxembourg)" title="Democratic Party (Luxembourg)">Democratic Party</a> (Luxembourg), <a href="/wiki/Venstre_(Denmark)" title="Venstre (Denmark)">Liberal Party</a> (Denmark) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_People%27s_Party_(Sweden)" title="Liberal People's Party (Sweden)">Liberal People's Party</a> (Sweden).</li>
<li>Blue is the colour of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> in Britain and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative Party of Canada</a>.</li>
<li>In the United States, television commentators use the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_states" title="Blue states">blue states</a>" for those states which traditionally vote for the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> in presidential elections, and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_states" title="Red states">red states</a>" for those which vote for the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Québec Province of Canada, the Blues are those who support sovereignty for Quebec, as opposed to the Federalists. It is the colour of the Parti québécois and the Parti libéral du Québec.</li>
<li>Blue is the colour of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Progressive_Party_of_Puerto_Rico" title="New Progressive Party of Puerto Rico">New Progressive Party of Puerto Rico</a>.</li>
<li>In Brazil, blue states are the ones in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_Social_Democratic_Party" title="Brazilian Social Democratic Party">Social Democratic Party</a> has the majority, in opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_Party_(Brazil)" title="Workers' Party (Brazil)">Workers' Party</a>, usually represented by red.</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Blue_law" title="Blue law">blue law</a> is a type of law, typically found in the United States and Canada, designed to enforce religious standards, particularly the observance of Sunday as a day of worship or rest, and a restriction on <a href="/wiki/Sunday_shopping" title="Sunday shopping">Sunday shopping</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_House" title="Blue House">Blue House</a> is the residence of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_South_Korea" title="President of South Korea">President of South Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
